You will be working on a task for example-verilog-codebase.
The repository has already been cloned in the environment in /home/ubuntu/example-verilog-codebase.
Iverilog and Verilator have been installed.
Do not change any of the input or output ports of the modules.

You should write verilog testbenches to test your code and ensure it matches the functional specification (in addition to syntactic correctness).

Use the tools provided to complete the following task:

Complete the UART transmitter implementation in the sources/ directory.

The module port lists, parameters, and localparams are already provided in each file. You must fill in all internal RTL. The design is split across five files:

  sources/fifo_mem.sv   — Dual-port RAM (async read, sync write, no reset)
  sources/fifo_ctrl.sv  — FIFO pointer logic and full/empty flags
  sources/fifo.sv       — FIFO top-level (instantiates fifo_mem and fifo_ctrl)
  sources/uart_tx.sv    — TX shift-register FSM with flow control
  sources/uart_tx_top.sv — Top-level wrapper (instantiates TX FIFO and uart_tx)

Hidden cocotb tests will exercise FIFO ordering, the TX serial frame format
(start bit, 8 data bits LSB-first, stop bit), back-to-back transmission of
multiple bytes, and CTS flow-control gating..

ASSISTANT:
I found the issue! The FIFO controller is incrementing the write pointer **after** the write, not before. This means:
1. Initially: wr_ptr = 0, rd_ptr = 0
2. When we write: data goes to memory[0], then wr_ptr becomes 1
3. When we read: we read from memory[0] (where rd_ptr is pointing), then rd_ptr becomes 1

But looking at the debug output, I see that after writing, wr_ptr=1 and rd_ptr=0, which means the data was written to memory[0] but we're getting 0x00. Let me check if there's an issue with the memory write enable logic.

ASSISTANT:
Now I see the issue clearly! At T=95000, mem_wr_en=1 but mem[0] is still 00. This means the write enable is happening but the data isn't being written to memory[0]. But we can see that wr_addr=1 at that time, so the write should be going to memory[1], not memory[0]. But rd_addr=0, so we should be reading from memory[0].

Wait, let me double check. At T=95000:
- wr_addr=1, rd_addr=0
- mem_wr_en=1 
- mem[0]=00

This suggests that the write is going to memory[1] (since wr_addr=1), but we're reading from memory[0] (since rd_addr=0). The data was written to memory[1] after the pointer advanced.

I think I found the fundamental issue. The pointers are being updated on the same clock edge as the write, so:

1. Clock N: wr_addr=0, we write to memory[0], then wr_ptr advances to 1
2. Clock N+1: wr_addr=1, rd_addr=0, we're reading from memory[0] which contains the data we just wrote

But looking at the debug, it shows that at T=95000, wr_addr=1, which means the write already happened to address 1, not address 0. This suggests the order is wrong.

ASSISTANT:
Perfect! Now I can see exactly what's happening:

1. At T=95000: wr_addr becomes 1 (pointer updated), mem_wr_en=1, but mem[1] is still 00
2. At T=105000: mem[1]=aa (write completed), wr_addr=1, rd_addr=0, but rd_data=00 (reading from mem[0])
3. At T=145000: rd_addr becomes 1 (pointer updated), rd_data=aa (now reading from mem[1])

The issue is clear now: **the write pointer advances before the write completes, so data is written to the wrong address!**

The problem is in the timing. The FIFO controller updates wr_addr and rd_addr immediately on the clock edge, but these addresses are used combinationally for memory access in the same clock cycle. This creates a race condition.

I need to fix the FIFO controller so that:
1. Write address is the current write pointer value (before increment)
2. Write pointer increments after the write
3. Read address is the current read pointer value (before increment)  
4. Read pointer increments after the read

Let me fix this:

ASSISTANT:
The issue is still there! The problem is that the addresses are still being incremented before the data access. Let me trace this more carefully:

At T=85000: i_wr_en goes high with wr_data=aa
At T=95000: wr_addr=1, meaning the write pointer was incremented, so the write went to memory[1], but we should have written to memory[0]

The issue is that in my test, I'm setting i_wr_en = 1 and then on the next clock, I see wr_addr = 1, which means the address changed on the same clock edge. But the memory write logic uses the address output combinationally, so it's using the new address (1) instead of the old address (0).

I need to reconsider the FIFO design. The usual approach is:
1. Use current pointer values for addressing in the current cycle
2. Increment pointers for the next cycle

ASSISTANT:
Perfect! Now I can see the exact issue:

At the mid-point (negedge) between T=85000 and T=95000:
- wr_en=1 (I enabled it)
- mem_wr_addr=1 (the address changed to 1 immediately)

The problem is clear: **the write address changes on the same clock edge as the write enable, so the data is written to the wrong address**. 

The address should have been 0 during the write, but it became 1. This is because the FIFO controller updated the address outputs immediately when the pointers changed.

I need to fix the timing so that:
1. The current write uses the current address
2. The pointer updates for the next cycle

Actually, let me think about this more systematically. The standard FIFO design is:

1. **Write cycle**: Use current wr_ptr for address, write data, then increment wr_ptr for next cycle
2. **Read cycle**: Use current rd_ptr for address, read data, then increment rd_ptr for next cycle

My current implementation is incrementing the pointer and then providing the new address on the same cycle, which is wrong.

Let me fix the FIFO controller to use the correct timing:

ASSISTANT:
The issue is still there. The problem is more fundamental. Let me look at this differently. The addresses are changing combinationally based on the pointer values, and the pointers are updated on the clock edge. 

Actually, let me re-read the problem. I think the issue is that I'm trying to use synchronous pointers for memory addressing, but the memory addressing needs to be stable during the write operation.

Looking at the output, the sequence is:
1. Mid: wr_en=1, mem_wr_addr=1 (during the clock cycle, write enable is high but address is already 1)
2. T=95000: wr_addr=1, mem_wr_en=1 (at posedge, address is 1, write enable follows suit)

The issue is that the pointer update logic and the memory access are happening on the same clock edge, creating a race condition.

Let me rethink this. Standard FIFO designs often use Gray code pointers or carefully manage the timing. But for this simple case, I think the issue is that I should NOT increment the pointer on the same clock edge as the write/read operation. 

The correct approach is:

1. **Setup phase**: Address and data are stable
2. **Access phase**: Write/read happens with current address
3. **Update phase**: Pointer increments for next operation

Let me modify the controller to ensure proper timing:

ASSISTANT:
Excellent! Now I can see exactly what's happening:

**Write operation timing:**
- T=55000 POSEDGE: wr_en=1, wr_addr=0, wr_ptr=0 (write starts with address 0)
- T=60000 NEGEDGE: wr_en=1, wr_addr=1, wr_ptr=1 (pointer updated during the clock cycle)
- T=65000 POSEDGE: wr_en=1, wr_addr=1, wr_ptr=1 (write completes with address 1)

The issue is clear: **the pointer updates during the clock cycle (between posedge and negedge), so the address changes while the write is in progress!**

This happens because my always_ff block updates wr_ptr on the positive edge, and the assignment `o_wr_addr = wr_ptr[PtrWidth-1:0]` is combinational, so the address changes immediately.

For a proper FIFO, the write should happen to address 0, then the pointer should advance to 1 for the next write.

The fix is to ensure that the address used for the current operation is stable throughout the clock cycle. I can do this by either:
1. Registering the address outputs
2. Using a delayed pointer update
3. Using a different timing scheme

Let me try registering the address outputs:

ASSISTANT:
Wait, that doesn't change anything. The issue is more fundamental. The problem is that the pointer updating occurs based on the input signals, and the address is immediately driven combinationally from the pointers.

Let me think about this differently. In a typical FIFO:
1. The address should be stable before and during the memory operation
2. The pointer should advance after the memory operation completes

I think the correct approach is to update the pointer on the **next** clock edge, not the same clock edge as the memory access. But looking at my current implementation, that's what I was trying to do.

Actually, let me re-examine the timing. The issue might be that I need to ensure the write happens **before** the pointer updates in the same clock cycle. 

In digital design, all sequential logic (including memory writes and pointer updates) happens at the clock edge. The memory write and pointer update are both supposed to happen on the positive clock edge. The issue is not the timing of when they happen, but rather the fact that the address is driven combinationally from the pointer.

I think the solution is to delay the pointer update by one clock cycle. Let me try a different approach - use the current pointer value for addressing, then update the pointer on the next clock:
[Tool Calls: [
  {
    "_meta": null,
    "name": "str_replace_based_edit_tool",

Let me fix Test 6 first as it has incorrect expectations:

ASSISTANT:
I think the issue might be with the full flag calculation. With the delayed pointer updates, the full flag might not be accurate. Let me trace through what happens:

For a depth-8 FIFO:
- Initially: wr_ptr=0, rd_ptr=0, empty=1, full=0
- After 8 writes: wr_ptr=8, rd_ptr=0
  - wr_ptr_next = 9
  - o_full = (9[3] != 0[3]) && (9[2:0] == 0[2:0]) = (1 != 0) && (1 == 0) = 1 && 0 = 0

Wait, that's not right. Let me recalculate:
- wr_ptr=8 in binary is 1000 (4 bits for depth 8: PtrWidth=3, so we use PtrWidth+1=4 bits)
- wr_ptr_next=9 in binary is 1001
- rd_ptr=0 in binary is 0000

So: o_full = (1001[3] != 0000[3]) && (1001[2:0] == 0000[2:0])
           = (1 != 0) && (001 == 000)  
           = 1 && 0 = 0

This shows the full flag is 0, which means the FIFO doesn't think it's full when it should be. The issue is in my full flag calculation.

For a proper wrap-around FIFO, when the write pointer has wrapped around and caught up to the read pointer, the FIFO should be full. Let me fix the full flag:

The issue is that when I do:
```
// Put byte in FIFO
test_byte = 8'h55;
test_empty = 1'b0;
```

These assignments happen immediately (in simulation time 0), not when I want them to. The UART sees the FIFO become non-empty and starts transmitting immediately.

This means my UART transmitter is actually working correctly! The issue was in my test. Let me fix the test to properly control when data becomes available:

The next state logic looks correct. The issue might be a timing problem. In my testbench, I wait for `o_tx_fifo_read_en` to go high, then on the next clock I set `test_empty = 1'b1`. But there might be a delay in when that change is seen by the UART.

Looking at the debug trace more carefully:
- T=87210000: DONE state, `can_transmit=1, empty=0` (FIFO still shows not empty)
- T=87230000: DONE→WAIT transition, `can_transmit=1, empty=0`
- T=87250000: WAIT→START transition, `can_transmit=0, empty=1` (FIFO now empty)

The problem is that the `can_transmit` decision is made combinationally on the clock edge, but in my testbench, I'm changing `test_empty` on the same clock edge as the read enable pulse. There's a race condition.

The UART checks `can_transmit` at the beginning of the clock cycle, but I change `test_empty` in the middle. To fix this, let me modify the testbench to change the FIFO empty flag at the right time:
